and the viewers' eye is hard to always perceive.]{.underline}

### AT: Baudrillard\-\--Perm

#### Our [postmodern simulation of life]{.underline} is a [turn]{.underline} against the simulacrum, forcing it into its own [mad proliferation]{.underline} that produces [differentiation]{.underline} rather than [sameness]{.underline}.

**Song 12**, University of California Riverside, (Mary, *Cyborg Dreams
in Asian American Transnationality: Transgression, Myth, Simulation,
Coalition*, University of California Riverside,
<https://escholarship.org/uc/item/53d2x7nd>) //CHC-DS üê±‚Äçüë§

If we contemplate how narrative-making is an art that simulates
identity, we might better appreciate the field of literary scholarship !
especially if we better understand the products of simulating identity
versus the consequences of representing identity. Jean Baudrillard
observes that our culture of simulation has become wholly reliant on
signifiers that have replaced "reality" with "hyperreality". Massumi
critiques [Baudrillard's notion of hyperreality as a perception that
leaves us in a sea of endlessly floating signifiers where "\[m\]eaning
has imploded"]{.underline} (Massumi, http://www.anu.edu.au). Massumi
asks, "[But do we really have no other choice than being a na√Øve realist
or being a sponge?]{.underline}" (ibid) [Instead, Massumi advocates
Deleuze's notion of simulacra as an alternative to "being a
sponge":]{.underline}

[The thrust of the process is not to become an equivalent of the "model"
but to]{.underline} **[turn against it]{.underline}** [and its world in
order to open a new space for the simulacrum's own]{.underline} **[mad
proliferation]{.underline}**. The simulacrum affirms its own difference.
[It is not an implosion, but a]{.underline}
**[differentiation]{.underline}**; it is an index not of absolute
proximity, but of galactic distances. (ibid)

Promulgating Deleuzian simulacra, Massumi rejects [Baudrillard's endless
sea of signifiers]{.underline} on the grounds that it [is **too
passive** a vision of the world]{.underline}. Massumi believes that
[Deleuze's figuration of simulacra lends the term more potency in that
the sea of signifiers we live in is not of endlessly nondiscriminate
labels, but rather, each singular signifier is a distinction **unto its
own**]{.underline}. [It is not about recognizing that we]{.underline}
(referent to things as well as persons) [are all really the same, it is
rather about recognizing that we are all really so incredibly
different.]{.underline}

In other words, [a]{.underline} **[postmodern literary simulation of
life]{.underline}** [can be]{.underline} **[indicative of the
turn]{.underline}** [that envisions potential political
change]{.underline}. Using Massumi's interpretation of Deleuzian
simulacra, I investigate how Choi simulates identity in her novel
American Woman by transgressing the boundaries between fiction and
non-fiction. By [imbricating]{.underline} her [story with non-fictional
information with fictive narrative]{.underline}, Choi [highlights the
permeability between what we perceive to be non-fiction and fiction in
order recognize our invested expectations in assimilation rather than
difference]{.underline}. She depicts how despite the U.S. media's
profound investment in revealing the "true" Pauline, not even Pauline
nor Jenny seem to know who this "true" Pauline is. On the other hand,
Choi showcases the media's absolute disinterest in Jenny; her narrative
emphasizes this fact by acutely focusing on Jenny's interior landscape.
In this way, Choi creates a paradoxical narrative that deconstructs
identity by thwarting our expectations of "discovering" any "true"
Pauline except through Jenny. In other words, Choi answers the real-life
question of Patty Hearst's identity as answerable only through the
fictive framework of Wendy Yoshimura's silenced narrative. In this way,
Choi' narrative reveals how **[identity]{.underline}** [is not found in
the assimilation of our desires but rather the]{.underline}
**[differences]{.underline}** [that we tend to overlook]{.underline}.
Choi chooses to [explore identity through the process of examining the
simulacra of U.S. media as commensurable to the simulacra of
fiction]{.underline}.

### AT: Identity-Based Ks\-\--Simulated Politics 

#### Representational identity politics are [pre-coded narratives]{.underline} that [recreate complicity]{.underline} to hegemonic oppression. Only by embracing the [logic of the cyborg]{.underline} in a [simulated politics]{.underline} can we construct space for our identities.

**Song 12**, University of California Riverside, (Mary, *Cyborg Dreams
in Asian American Transnationality: Transgression, Myth, Simulation,
Coalition*, University of California Riverside,
<https://escholarship.org/uc/item/53d2x7nd>) //CHC-DS üê±‚Äçüë§

It is this severance from any original story that supports Haraway's
notion of simulating identity over representing identity. When Haraway
states that "[\[t\]he cyborg is not subject to]{.underline} Foucault's
[biopolitics; the cyborg]{.underline} **[simulates
politics]{.underline}**, [a much more potent field of
operations]{.underline}" (Haraway 164), she recognizes how [simulation
politics does not recapitulate the same kind of centralizing logic of
power that representation politics is susceptible to]{.underline}.
[Foucault's biopolitics describes the]{.underline} **[technologies of
oppression]{.underline}** [that the nation-state has exerted over marked
bodies in the guise of population hygiene.]{.underline} One step away
from eugenics, biopolitics organizes the population under the logic of
caring for the population such that a body is no longer an individual's
right but that one body is in the rightful care of everyone else. In
this way, [hegemonic power succeeds in representing the needs of a body
such that its oppression of that body is seemingly
justified]{.underline}. [For biopolitics to continue, we must
remain]{.underline} **[complicit]{.underline}** [to the myth that truth
is a representable phenomenon and that there lies an originary truth for
all bodies]{.underline}. Representational politics of identity for
marked bodies recapitulate this logic by perpetuating the myth that
certain bodies can represent a particular originary truth. [Relying on
the body as image, such a politics forces us to commit to a]{.underline}
**[pre-coded narrative]{.underline}** [that applies to certain multiple
bodies. In both cases of the nation-state as well as minority politics,
representational politics ultimately **substantiates** the kind of
boundaries that centralize and marginalize]{.underline}.

A **[simulational politics of identity]{.underline}** [based on
the]{.underline} **[logic of the cyborg]{.underline}** [resists the
centralization of power in that it rejects the myth of any originary
truth]{.underline}. [Instead, the simulation politics of the cyborg is
about producing its]{.underline} **[own truths]{.underline}** [based on
the recognition and transgression of the boundaries that sustain
hegemonic mythologies about truth and the body]{.underline}. According
to Haraway, [the body is no longer a unified, coherent and pure
entity]{.underline}. Subsequently, [to construct our identities based on
such notions of clarity and purity only serve to sustain
our]{.underline} **[complicit subservience]{.underline}** [to an
informatics of domination through the hegemonic technologization of our
bodies]{.underline}. In order to escape these systems of oppression,
[the cyborg offers its]{.underline} **[own
technologization]{.underline}** [of the body such that we embrace
the]{.underline} **[hybrid conditions of our bodies]{.underline}**
[and]{.underline} the **[identities]{.underline}** [we consciously
construct]{.underline} around them. [Simulating identity]{.underline},
then, [is about recognizing that there is no original story, and
that]{.underline} **[the real story is the story that is unfolding right
in front of us.]{.underline}** **[Identity cannot be represented nor can
it be discovered]{.underline}**. Rather, [identity unravels]{.underline}
**[experientially]{.underline}** [such that it is recognized
processually as we transgress across boundaries that reveal the
constructed conditions of subjectivity]{.underline}. Haraway's cyborg
articulates the profound stakes in which such an alternate politics of
identity are invested in when she states: "[The stakes in the border war
have been the territories of production, reproduction, and
imagination]{.underline}." (Haraway 151)
